/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Notes</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<div style="text-align: left;">\n<div class="plainlist"><ul><li><sup>a</sup> <a href="/wiki/Boston_University" title="Boston University">Boston University</a> (see <a href="#Continuing_experimentation">below</a>).</li><li><sup>b</sup> See <a href="#Telephone">below</a>.</li><li><sup>c</sup> Two died soon after birth.</li></ul></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Alexander Graham Bell</b> (March 3, 1847&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 August 2, 1922)<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> was a  <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>-born<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;N 2&#93;</a></sup> scientist, inventor, engineer, and innovator who is credited with inventing and patenting the first practical <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a>. He also founded the <a href="/wiki/American_Telephone_and_Telegraph_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="American Telephone and Telegraph Company">American Telephone and Telegraph Company</a> (AT&amp;T) in 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell\'s father, grandfather, and brother had all been associated with work on <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a> and speech and both his mother and wife were deaf, profoundly influencing Bell\'s life\'s work.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His research on hearing and speech further led him to experiment with hearing devices which eventually culminated in Bell being awarded the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_patent_law" title="United States patent law">U.S. patent</a> for the telephone in 1876.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;N 3&#93;</a></sup> Bell considered his invention an intrusion on his real work as a scientist and refused to have a telephone in his study.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;N 4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many other inventions marked Bell\'s later life, including groundbreaking work in <a href="/wiki/Free-space_optical_communication" title="Free-space optical communication">optical telecommunications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrofoil" title="Hydrofoil">hydrofoils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aeronautics" title="Aeronautics">aeronautics</a>. Although Bell was not one of the 33 founders<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, he had a strong influence on the magazine while serving as the second president from January 7, 1898, until 1903.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#First_invention"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">First invention</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#First_experiments_with_sound"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">First experiments with sound</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Family_tragedy"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Family tragedy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Canada"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Canada</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Work_with_the_deaf"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Work with the deaf</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Continuing_experimentation"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Continuing experimentation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#The_telephone"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">The telephone</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#The_race_to_the_patent_office"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">The race to the patent office</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Later_developments"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Later developments</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Competitors"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Competitors</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Family_life"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Family life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Later_inventions"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Later inventions</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Photophone"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Photophone</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Metal_detector"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Metal detector</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Hydrofoils"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Hydrofoils</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Aeronautics"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Aeronautics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Eugenics"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Eugenics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Legacy_and_honors"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy and honors</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Honorary_degrees"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Honorary degrees</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Innovators_awarded_in_his_name"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Innovators awarded in his name</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Portrayal_in_film_and_television"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Portrayal in film and television</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Patents"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Patents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Multimedia"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Multimedia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>\n<p>Alexander Bell was born in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland, on March 3, 1847.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The family home was at 16 South Charlotte Street, and has a stone inscription marking it as Alexander Graham Bell\'s birthplace. He had two brothers: Melville James Bell (1845\xe2\x80\x9370) and Edward Charles Bell (1848\xe2\x80\x9367), both of whom would die of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> His father was Professor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Melville_Bell" title="Alexander Melville Bell">Alexander Melville Bell</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetician</a>, and his mother was Eliza Grace (n\xc3\xa9e Symonds).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Born as just "Alexander Bell", at age 10, he made a plea to his father to have a middle name like his two brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;N 5&#93;</a></sup> For his 11th birthday, his father acquiesced and allowed him to adopt the name "Graham", chosen out of respect for Alexander Graham, a <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> being treated by his father who had become a family friend.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> To close relatives and friends he remained "Aleck".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199017\xe2\x80\x9319_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199017\xe2\x80\x9319-24">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_invention">First invention</span></h3>\n<p>As a child, young Bell displayed a natural curiosity about his world, resulting in gathering botanical specimens as well as experimenting even at an early age. His best friend was Ben Herdman, a neighbour whose family operated a flour mill, the scene of many forays. Young Bell asked what needed to be done at the mill. He was told wheat had to be dehusked through a laborious process and at the age of 12, Bell built a homemade device that combined rotating paddles with sets of nail brushes, creating a simple dehusking machine that was put into operation and used steadily for a number of years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199016_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199016-25">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In return, Ben\'s father John Herdman gave both boys the run of a small workshop in which to "invent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199016_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199016-25">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From his early years, Bell showed a sensitive nature and a talent for art, poetry, and music that was encouraged by his mother. With no formal training, he mastered the piano and became the family\'s pianist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray20068_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray20068-26">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Despite being normally quiet and introspective, he revelled in mimicry and "voice tricks" akin to <a href="/wiki/Ventriloquism" title="Ventriloquism">ventriloquism</a> that continually entertained family guests during their occasional visits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray20068_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray20068-26">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Bell was also deeply affected by his mother\'s gradual deafness (she began to lose her hearing when he was 12), and learned a manual finger language so he could sit at her side and tap out silently the conversations swirling around the family parlour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray20069_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray20069-27">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He also developed a technique of speaking in clear, modulated tones directly into his mother\'s forehead wherein she would hear him with reasonable clarity.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Bell\'s preoccupation with his mother\'s deafness led him to study <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a>.\n</p><p>His family was long associated with the teaching of elocution: his grandfather, Alexander Bell, in London, his uncle in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, and his father, in Edinburgh, were all elocutionists. His father published a variety of works on the subject, several of which are still well known, especially his <i>The Standard Elocutionist</i> (1860),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray20068_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray20068-26">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> which appeared in Edinburgh in 1868. <i>The Standard Elocutionist</i> appeared in 168 British editions and sold over a quarter of a million copies in the United States alone. In this treatise, his father explains his methods of how to instruct <a href="/wiki/Deaf-mute" title="Deaf-mute">deaf-mutes</a> (as they were then known) to articulate words and read other people\'s lip movements to decipher meaning. Bell\'s father taught him and his brothers not only to write <a href="/wiki/Visible_Speech" title="Visible Speech">Visible Speech</a> but to identify any symbol and its accompanying sound.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Bell became so proficient that he became a part of his father\'s public demonstrations and astounded audiences with his abilities. He could decipher Visible Speech representing virtually every language, including <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, accurately reciting written tracts without any prior knowledge of their pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" width="30" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Alexander_Graham_Bell#Earlier_photos_of_Bell.27s_ancestral_family" class="extiw" title="commons:Category:Alexander Graham Bell">a young Bell with his parents</a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>\n<p>As a young child, Bell, like his brothers, received his early schooling at home from his father. At an early age, he was enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_High_School_(Edinburgh)" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal High School (Edinburgh)">Royal High School</a>, Edinburgh, Scotland, which he left at the age of 15, having completed only the first four forms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay199731_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay199731-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> His school record was undistinguished, marked by absenteeism and lacklustre grades. His main interest remained in the sciences, especially biology while he treated other school subjects with indifference, to the dismay of his demanding father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray200611_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray200611-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Upon leaving school, Bell travelled to London to live with his grandfather, Alexander Bell. During the year he spent with his grandfather, a love of learning was born, with long hours spent in serious discussion and study. The elder Bell took great efforts to have his young pupil learn to speak clearly and with conviction, the attributes that his pupil would need to become a teacher himself.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> At the age of 16, Bell secured a position as a "pupil-teacher" of <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a> and music, in Weston House Academy at <a href="/wiki/Elgin,_Moray" title="Elgin, Moray">Elgin, Moray</a>, Scotland. Although he was enrolled as a student in Latin and Greek, he instructed classes himself in return for board and \xc2\xa310 per session.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199037_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199037-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The following year, he attended the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>; joining his older brother Melville who had enrolled there the previous year. In 1868, not long before he departed for Canada with his family, Bell completed his matriculation exams and was accepted for admission to <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_experiments_with_sound">First experiments with sound</span></h3>\n<p>His father encouraged Bell\'s interest in speech and, in 1863, took his sons to see a unique <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automaton</a> developed by Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wheatstone" title="Charles Wheatstone">Charles Wheatstone</a> based on the earlier work of <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_von_Kempelen" title="Wolfgang von Kempelen">Baron Wolfgang von Kempelen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The rudimentary "mechanical man" simulated a human voice. Bell was fascinated by the machine and after he obtained a copy of von Kempelen\'s book, published in German, and had laboriously translated it, he and his older brother Melville built their own automaton head. Their father, highly interested in their project, offered to pay for any supplies and spurred the boys on with the enticement of a "big prize" if they were successful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> While his brother constructed the throat and <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>, Bell tackled the more difficult task of recreating a realistic skull. His efforts resulted in a remarkably lifelike head that could "speak", albeit only a few words.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200525-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The boys would carefully adjust the "lips" and when a <a href="/wiki/Bellows" title="Bellows">bellows</a> forced air through the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertebrate trachea">windpipe</a>, a very recognizable "Mama" ensued, to the delight of neighbours who came to see the Bell invention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757\xe2\x80\x939_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie19757\xe2\x80\x939-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Intrigued by the results of the automaton, Bell continued to experiment with a live subject, the family\'s <a href="/wiki/Skye_Terrier" title="Skye Terrier">Skye Terrier</a>, "Trouve".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie19759_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie19759-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After he taught it to growl continuously, Bell would reach into its mouth and manipulate the dog\'s lips and <a href="/wiki/Vocal_cords" title="Vocal cords">vocal cords</a> to produce a crude-sounding "Ow ah oo ga ma ma". With little convincing, visitors believed his dog could articulate "How are you, grandma?" Indicative of his playful nature, his experiments convinced onlookers that they saw a "talking dog".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200530_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200530-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> These initial forays into experimentation with sound led Bell to undertake his first serious work on the transmission of sound, using <a href="/wiki/Tuning_fork" title="Tuning fork">tuning forks</a> to explore <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">resonance</a>.\n</p><p>At age 19, Bell wrote a report on his work and sent it to philologist <a href="/wiki/Alexander_John_Ellis" title="Alexander John Ellis">Alexander Ellis</a>, a colleague of his father (who would later be portrayed as Professor Henry Higgins in <i><a href="/wiki/Pygmalion_(play)" title="Pygmalion (play)">Pygmalion</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200530_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200530-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Ellis immediately wrote back indicating that the experiments were similar to existing work in Germany, and also lent Bell a copy of <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a>\'s work, <i>The Sensations of Tone as a Physiological Basis for the Theory of Music</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShulman200846_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShulman200846-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dismayed to find that groundbreaking work had already been undertaken by Helmholtz who had conveyed vowel sounds by means of a similar tuning fork "contraption", Bell pored over the German scientist\'s book. Working from his own erroneous mistranslation of a French edition,<sup id="cite_ref-DCB_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCB-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Bell fortuitously then made a deduction that would be the underpinning of all his future work on transmitting sound, reporting: "Without knowing much about the subject, it seemed to me that if vowel sounds could be produced by electrical means, so could consonants, so could articulate speech." He also later remarked: "I thought that Helmholtz had done it&#160;... and that my failure was due only to my ignorance of electricity. It was a valuable blunder&#160;... If I had been able to read German in those days, I might never have commenced my experiments!"<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200531_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200531-42">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShulman200846\xe2\x80\x9348_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShulman200846\xe2\x80\x9348-43">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;N 6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_tragedy">Family tragedy</span></h3>\n<p>In 1865, when the Bell family moved to London,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Bell returned to Weston House as an assistant master and, in his spare hours, continued experiments on sound using a minimum of laboratory equipment. Bell concentrated on experimenting with electricity to convey sound and later installed a <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a> wire from his room in Somerset College to that of a friend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199045_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199045-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Throughout late 1867, his health faltered mainly through exhaustion. His younger brother, Edward "Ted," was similarly bed-ridden, suffering from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. While Bell recovered (by then referring to himself in correspondence as "A. G. Bell") and served the next year as an instructor at Somerset College, <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>, England, his brother\'s condition deteriorated. Edward would never recover. Upon his brother\'s death, Bell returned home in 1867. His older brother Melville had married and moved out. With aspirations to obtain a degree at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>, Bell considered his next years as preparation for the degree examinations, devoting his spare time at his family\'s residence to studying.\n</p><p>Helping his father in Visible Speech demonstrations and lectures brought Bell to Susanna E. Hull\'s private school for the deaf in <a href="/wiki/South_Kensington" title="South Kensington">South Kensington</a>, London. His first two pupils were <a href="/wiki/Deaf-mute" title="Deaf-mute">deaf-mute</a> girls who made remarkable progress under his tutelage. While his older brother seemed to achieve success on many fronts including opening his own elocution school, applying for a patent on an invention, and starting a family, Bell continued as a teacher. However, in May 1870, Melville died from complications due to tuberculosis, causing a family crisis. His father had also suffered a debilitating illness earlier in life and had been restored to health by a convalescence in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>. Bell\'s parents embarked upon a long-planned move when they realized that their remaining son was also sickly. Acting decisively, Alexander Melville Bell asked Bell to arrange for the sale of all the family property,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199067\xe2\x80\x9328_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199067\xe2\x80\x9328-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;N 7&#93;</a></sup> conclude all of his brother\'s affairs (Bell took over his last student, curing a pronounced lisp),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199068_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199068-49">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and join his father and mother in setting out for the "<a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>". Reluctantly, Bell also had to conclude a relationship with Marie Eccleston, who, as he had surmised, was not prepared to leave England with him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200533_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200533-50">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site" title="Bell Homestead National Historic Site">Bell Homestead National Historic Site</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site,_Brantford,_Ontario,_Canada,_incl._Visitor_Ctr,_Henderson_Home,_Carriage_House_and_Dreaming_Place_IMG_0039_09.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/The_Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site%2C_Brantford%2C_Ontario%2C_Canada%2C_incl._Visitor_Ctr%2C_Henderson_Home%2C_Carriage_House_and_Dreaming_Place_IMG_0039_09.JPG/330px-The_Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site%2C_Brantford%2C_Ontario%2C_Canada%2C_incl._Visitor_Ctr%2C_Henderson_Home%2C_Carriage_House_and_Dreaming_Place_IMG_0039_09.JPG
