2x" data-file-width="2925" data-file-height="1993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site,_Brantford,_Ontario,_Canada,_incl._Visitor_Ctr,_Henderson_Home,_Carriage_House_and_Dreaming_Place_IMG_0039_09.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site" title="Bell Homestead National Historic Site">Melville House</a>, the Bells\' first home in North America, now a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Sites_of_Canada" title="National Historic Sites of Canada">National Historic Site of Canada</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1870, aged 23, Bell, together with Bell\'s brother\'s widow, Caroline Margaret Ottaway,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay199750_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay199750-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and his parents travelled on the SS <i>Nestorian</i> to Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie197510_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie197510-52">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> After landing at <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, the Bells transferred to another steamer to <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> and then boarded a train to <a href="/wiki/Paris,_Ontario" title="Paris, Ontario">Paris, Ontario</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray200621_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray200621-53">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> to stay with the Reverend Thomas Henderson, a family friend. After a brief stay with the Hendersons, the Bell family purchased a farm of 10.5 acres (42,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) at Tutelo Heights (now called Tutela Heights), near <a href="/wiki/Brantford" title="Brantford">Brantford</a>, Ontario. The property consisted of an orchard, large farmhouse, stable, pigsty, hen-house, and a <a href="/wiki/Carriage_house" title="Carriage house">carriage house</a>, which bordered the <a href="/wiki/Grand_River_(Ontario)" title="Grand River (Ontario)">Grand River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay199761_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay199761-54">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;N 8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the homestead, Bell set up his own workshop in the converted carriage house near to what he called his "dreaming place",<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> a large hollow nestled in trees at the back of the property above the river.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200534_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200534-58">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Despite his frail condition upon arriving in Canada, Bell found the climate and environs to his liking, and rapidly improved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay199762_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay199762-59">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;N 9&#93;</a></sup> He continued his interest in the study of the human voice and when he discovered the <a href="/wiki/Six_Nations_40,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Six Nations 40, Ontario">Six Nations Reserve</a> across the river at <a href="/wiki/Onondaga_(village)" title="Onondaga (village)">Onondaga</a>, he learned the <a href="/wiki/Mohawk_language" title="Mohawk language">Mohawk language</a> and translated its unwritten vocabulary into Visible Speech symbols. For his work, Bell was awarded the title of Honorary Chief and participated in a ceremony where he donned a <a href="/wiki/Mohawk_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohawk nation">Mohawk</a> headdress and danced traditional dances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200535_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200535-61">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;N 10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After setting up his workshop, Bell continued experiments based on Helmholtz\'s work with electricity and sound.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWing198010_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWing198010-63">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> He also modified a <a href="/wiki/Pump_organ" title="Pump organ">melodeon</a> (a type of pump organ) so that it could transmit its music electrically over a distance.<sup id="cite_ref-Waldi-undated_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waldi-undated-64">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Once the family was settled in, both Bell and his father made plans to establish a teaching practice and in 1871, he accompanied his father to Montreal, where Melville was offered a position to teach his System of Visible Speech.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_with_the_deaf">Work with the deaf</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf,_Boston,_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg/220px-Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg/330px-Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg/440px-Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf%2C_Boston%2C_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2317" data-file-height="1582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_at_the_Pemberton_Avenue_School_for_the_Deaf,_Boston,_from_the_Library_of_Congress._00837v.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell, top right, providing <a href="/wiki/Pedagogy" title="Pedagogy">pedagogical instruction</a> to teachers at the Boston School for Deaf Mutes, 1871. Throughout his life, he referred to himself as "a teacher of the deaf".</div></div></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" width="30" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Alexander_Graham_Bell#Alexander_Graham_Bell_-General_photos_with_other_people_or_groups" class="extiw" title="commons:Category:Alexander Graham Bell">the <b>Scott Circle School for Deaf Children</b></a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Bell\'s father was invited by Sarah Fuller, principal of the Boston School for Deaf Mutes (which continues today as the public <a href="/wiki/Horace_Mann_School_for_the_Deaf_and_Hard_of_Hearing" title="Horace Mann School for the Deaf and Hard of Hearing">Horace Mann School for the Deaf</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199074_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199074-65">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> in Boston, Massachusetts, United States, to introduce the Visible Speech System by providing training for Fuller\'s instructors, but he declined the post in favour of his son. Travelling to Boston in April 1871, Bell proved successful in training the school\'s instructors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETown198812_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETown198812-66">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> He was subsequently asked to repeat the programme at the <a href="/wiki/American_School_for_the_Deaf" title="American School for the Deaf">American Asylum for Deaf-mutes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hartford,_Connecticut" title="Hartford, Connecticut">Hartford, Connecticut</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Clarke_School_for_the_Deaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarke School for the Deaf">Clarke School for the Deaf</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton, Massachusetts</a>.\n</p><p>Returning home to Brantford after six months abroad, Bell continued his experiments with his "harmonic telegraph".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;N 11&#93;</a></sup> The basic concept behind his device was that messages could be sent through a single wire if each message was transmitted at a different pitch, but work on both the transmitter and receiver was needed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200539_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200539-69">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unsure of his future, he first contemplated returning to London to complete his studies, but decided to return to Boston as a teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie197514_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie197514-70">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> His father helped him set up his private practice by contacting <a href="/wiki/Gardiner_Greene_Hubbard" title="Gardiner Greene Hubbard">Gardiner Greene Hubbard</a>, the president of the Clarke School for the Deaf for a recommendation. Teaching his father\'s system, in October 1872, Alexander Bell opened his "School of Vocal Physiology and Mechanics of Speech" in Boston, which attracted a large number of deaf pupils, with his first class numbering 30 students.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie197515_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie197515-71">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETown198812\xe2\x80\x9313_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETown198812\xe2\x80\x9313-72">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> While he was working as a private tutor, one of his pupils was <a href="/wiki/Helen_Keller" title="Helen Keller">Helen Keller</a>, who came to him as a young child unable to see, hear, or speak. She was later to say that Bell dedicated his life to the penetration of that "inhuman silence which separates and estranges".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie197517_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie197517-73">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In 1893, Keller performed the sod-breaking ceremony for the construction of Bell\'s new <a href="/wiki/Volta_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="Volta Bureau">Volta Bureau</a>, dedicated to "the increase and diffusion of knowledge relating to the deaf".<sup id="cite_ref-Schoenherr2005_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoenherr2005-74">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several influential people of the time, including Bell, viewed deafness as something that should be eradicated, and also believed that with resources and effort, they could teach the deaf to speak and avoid the use of <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a>, thus enabling their integration within the wider society from which many were often being excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Owing to his efforts to suppress the teaching of sign language, Bell is often viewed negatively by those embracing <a href="/wiki/Deaf_culture" title="Deaf culture">Deaf culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuing_experimentation">Continuing experimentation</span></h2>\n<p>In the following year, Bell became professor of Vocal Physiology and Elocution at the <a href="/wiki/Boston_University" title="Boston University">Boston University</a> School of Oratory. During this period, he alternated between Boston and Brantford, spending summers in his Canadian home. At Boston University, Bell was "swept up" by the excitement engendered by the many scientists and inventors residing in the city. He continued his research in sound and endeavored to find a way to transmit musical notes and articulate speech, but although absorbed by his experiments, he found it difficult to devote enough time to experimentation. While days and evenings were occupied by his teaching and private classes, Bell began to stay awake late into the night, running experiment after experiment in rented facilities at his boarding house. Keeping "night owl" hours, he worried that his work would be discovered and took great pains to lock up his notebooks and laboratory equipment. Bell had a specially made table where he could place his notes and equipment inside a locking cover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETown198815_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETown198815-78">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Worse still, his health deteriorated as he suffered severe headaches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200539_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200539-69">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Returning to Boston in fall 1873, Bell made a fateful decision to concentrate on his experiments in sound.\n</p><p>Deciding to give up his lucrative private Boston practice, Bell retained only two students, six-year-old "Georgie" Sanders, deaf from birth, and 15-year-old <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel Hubbard</a>. Each pupil would play an important role in the next developments. George\'s father, Thomas Sanders, a wealthy businessman, offered Bell a place to stay in nearby <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Massachusetts" title="Salem, Massachusetts">Salem</a> with Georgie\'s grandmother, complete with a room to "experiment". Although the offer was made by George\'s mother and followed the year-long arrangement in 1872 where her son and his nurse had moved to quarters next to Bell\'s boarding house, it was clear that Mr. Sanders was backing the proposal. The arrangement was for teacher and student to continue their work together, with free room and board thrown in.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETown198816_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETown198816-79">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Mabel was a bright, attractive girl who was ten years Bell\'s junior but became the object of his affection. Having lost her hearing after a near-fatal bout of <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">scarlet fever</a> close to her fifth birthday,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;N 12&#93;</a></sup> she had learned to <a href="/wiki/Lip_reading" title="Lip reading">read lips</a> but her father, <a href="/wiki/Gardiner_Greene_Hubbard" title="Gardiner Greene Hubbard">Gardiner Greene Hubbard</a>, Bell\'s benefactor and personal friend, wanted her to work directly with her teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_telephone">The telephone</span></h2>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em"><caption>External audio</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">\n<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cbc.ca/archives/entry/alexander-graham-bell-and-thomas-watson">Alexander Graham Bell and Thomas Watson</a>, 26:58, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">CBC Archives</a><sup id="cite_ref-CBC_archives_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBC_archives-84">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Invention_of_the_telephone" title="Invention of the telephone">Invention of the telephone</a></div>\n<p>By 1874, Bell\'s initial work on the harmonic telegraph had entered a formative stage, with progress made both at his new Boston "laboratory" (a rented facility) and at his family home in Canada a big success.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;N 13&#93;</a></sup> While working that summer in Brantford, Bell experimented with a "<a href="/wiki/Phonautograph" title="Phonautograph">phonautograph</a>", a pen-like machine that could draw shapes of sound waves on smoked glass by tracing their vibrations. Bell thought it might be possible to generate undulating electrical currents that corresponded to sound waves.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Bell also thought that multiple metal reeds tuned to different frequencies like a harp would be able to convert the undulating currents back into sound. But he had no working model to demonstrate the feasibility of these ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthews199921_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthews199921-87">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1874, telegraph message traffic was rapidly expanding and in the words of <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">Western Union</a> President <a href="/wiki/William_Orton_(businessman)" title="William Orton (businessman)">William Orton</a>, had become "the nervous system of commerce". Antonio Meucci sent a telephone model and technical details to the Western Union telegraph company but failed to win a meeting with executives. When he asked for his materials to be returned, in 1874, he was told they had been lost. Two years later Bell, who shared a laboratory with Meucci, filed a patent for a telephone, became a celebrity and made a lucrative deal with Western Union. Meucci sued and was nearing victory - the supreme court agreed to hear the case and fraud charges were initiated against Bell - when the Florentine died in 1889. The legal action died with him.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  Orton had contracted with inventors <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elisha_Gray" title="Elisha Gray">Elisha Gray</a> to find a way to send multiple telegraph messages on each telegraph line to avoid the great cost of constructing new lines.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> When Bell mentioned to Gardiner Hubbard and Thomas Sanders that he was working on a method of sending multiple tones on a telegraph wire using a multi-reed device, the two wealthy patrons began to financially support Bell\'s experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETown198817_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETown198817-90">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Patent matters would be handled by Hubbard\'s <a href="/wiki/Patent_attorney" title="Patent attorney">patent attorney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Pollok" title="Anthony Pollok">Anthony Pollok</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 1875, Bell and Pollok visited the scientist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Henry" title="Joseph Henry">Joseph Henry</a>, who was then director of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>, and asked Henry\'s advice on the electrical multi-reed apparatus that Bell hoped would transmit the human voice by telegraph. Henry replied that Bell had "the germ of a great invention". When Bell said that he did not have the necessary knowledge, Henry replied, "Get it!" That declaration greatly encouraged Bell to keep trying, even though he did not have the equipment needed to continue his experiments, nor the ability to create a working model of his ideas. However, a chance meeting in 1874 between Bell and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Watson" title="Thomas A. Watson">Thomas A. Watson</a>, an experienced electrical designer and mechanic at the electrical machine shop of Charles Williams, changed all that.\n</p><p>With financial support from Sanders and Hubbard, Bell hired Thomas Watson as his assistant,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;N 14&#93;</a></sup> and the two of them experimented with <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_telegraphy" title="Acoustic telegraphy">acoustic telegraphy</a>. On June 2, 1875, Watson accidentally plucked one of the reeds and Bell, at the receiving end of the wire, heard the overtones of the reed; overtones that would be necessary for transmitting speech. That demonstrated to Bell that only one reed or armature was necessary, not multiple reeds. This led to the "gallows" <a href="/wiki/Sound-powered_telephone" title="Sound-powered telephone">sound-powered telephone</a>, which could transmit indistinct, voice-like sounds, but not clear speech.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_race_to_the_patent_office">The race to the patent office</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elisha_Gray_and_Alexander_Bell_telephone_controversy" title="Elisha Gray and Alexander Bell telephone controversy">Elisha Gray and Alexander Bell telephone controversy</a></div>\n<p>In 1875, Bell developed an <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Acoustic Telegraph">acoustic telegraph</a> and drew up a <a href="/wiki/Patent_application" title="Patent application">patent application</a> for it. Since he had agreed to share U.S. profits with his investors Gardiner Hubbard and Thomas Sanders, Bell requested that an associate in Ontario, <a href="/wiki/George_Brown_(Canadian_politician)" title="George Brown (Canadian politician)">George Brown</a>, attempt to patent it in Britain, instructing his lawyers to apply for a patent in the U.S. only after they received word from Britain (Britain would issue patents only for discoveries not previously patented elsewhere).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990158\xe2\x80\x93159_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990158\xe2\x80\x93159-95">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg/220px-TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg" width="220" height="306" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg/330px-TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg/440px-TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="864" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TelephonePatentDrawingBell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Graham Bell\'s telephone patent<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> drawing, March 7, 1876</div></div></div>\n<p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Elisha_Gray" title="Elisha Gray">Elisha Gray</a> was also experimenting with acoustic telegraphy and thought of a way to transmit speech using a water transmitter. On February 14, 1876, Gray filed a <a href="/wiki/Patent_caveat" title="Patent caveat">caveat</a> with the U.S. Patent Office for a telephone design that used a water transmitter. That same morning, Bell\'s lawyer filed Bell\'s application with the patent office. There is considerable debate about who arrived first and Gray later challenged the primacy of Bell\'s patent. Bell was in Boston on February 14 and did not arrive in Washington until February 26.\n</p><p>Bell\'s patent 174,465, was issued to Bell on March 7, 1876, by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">U.S. Patent Office</a>. Bell\'s patent covered "the method of, and apparatus for, transmitting vocal or other sounds telegraphically ... by causing electrical undulations, similar in form to the vibrations of the air accompanying the said vocal or other sound"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199912\xe2\x80\x9313_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199912\xe2\x80\x9313-97">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;N 15&#93;</a></sup> Bell returned to Boston the same day and the next day resumed work, drawing in his notebook a diagram similar to that in Gray\'s patent caveat.\n</p><p>On March 10, 1876, three days after his patent was issued, Bell succeeded in getting his telephone to work, using a liquid transmitter similar to Gray\'s design. Vibration of the diaphragm caused a needle to vibrate in the water, varying the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical resistance">electrical resistance</a> in the circuit. When Bell spoke the sentence "Mr. Watson\xe2\x80\x94Come here\xe2\x80\x94I want to see you" into the liquid transmitter,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Watson, listening at the receiving end in an adjoining room, heard the words clearly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199912_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199912-100">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although Bell was, and still is, accused of stealing the telephone from Gray,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShulman2008211_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShulman2008211-101">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Bell used Gray\'s water transmitter design only after Bell\'s patent had been granted, and only as a <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_concept" title="Proof of concept">proof of concept</a> scientific experiment,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvenson200099_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvenson200099-102">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> to prove to his own satisfaction that intelligible "articulate speech" (Bell\'s words) could be electrically transmitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvenson200098_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvenson200098-103">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> After March 1876, Bell focused on improving the electromagnetic telephone and never used Gray\'s liquid transmitter in public demonstrations or commercial use.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvenson2000100_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvenson2000100-104">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The question of priority for the variable resistance feature of the telephone was raised by the examiner before he approved Bell\'s patent application. He told Bell that his claim for the variable resistance feature was also described in Gray\'s caveat. Bell pointed to a variable resistance device in Bell\'s previous application in which Bell described a cup of mercury, not water. Bell had filed the mercury application at the patent office a year earlier on February 25, 1875, long before Elisha Gray described the water device. In addition, Gray abandoned his caveat, and because he did not contest Bell\'s priority, the examiner approved Bell\'s patent on March 3, 1876. Gray had reinvented the variable resistance telephone, but Bell was the first to write down the idea and the first to test it in a telephone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvenson200081\xe2\x80\x9382_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvenson200081\xe2\x80\x9382-105">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Patent_clerk" class="mw-redirect" title="Patent clerk">patent examiner</a>, Zenas Fisk Wilber, later stated in an <a href="/wiki/Affidavit" title="Affidavit">affidavit</a> that he was an alcoholic who was much in debt to Bell\'s lawyer, <a href="/wiki/Marcellus_Bailey" title="Marcellus Bailey">Marcellus Bailey</a>, with whom he had served in the Civil War. He claimed he showed Gray\'s patent caveat to Bailey. Wilber also claimed (after Bell arrived in Washington D.C. from Boston) that he showed Gray\'s caveat to Bell and that Bell paid him $100. Bell claimed they discussed the patent only in general terms, although in a letter to Gray, Bell admitted that he learned some of the technical details. Bell denied in an affidavit that he ever gave Wilber any money.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_developments">Later developments</span></h3>\n<p>Continuing his experiments in Brantford, Bell brought home a working model of his telephone. On August 3, 1876, from the telegraph office in <a href="/wiki/County_of_Brant" title="County of Brant">Mount Pleasant</a> five miles (eight&#160;km) away from Brantford, Bell sent a tentative telegram indicating that he was ready. With curious onlookers packed into the office as witnesses, faint voices were heard replying. The following night, he amazed guests as well as his family when a message was received at the Bell home from Brantford, four miles (six km) distant, along an improvised wire strung up along telegraph lines and fences, and laid through a tunnel. This time, guests at the household distinctly heard people in Brantford reading and singing. These experiments clearly proved that the telephone could work over long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199914_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199914-107">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg/220px-Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg/330px-Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg/440px-Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg 2x" data-file-width="445" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Telephone_in_Newyork.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell at the opening of the long-distance line from New York to Chicago in 1892</div></div></div>\n<p>Bell and his partners, Hubbard and Sanders, offered to sell the patent outright to Western Union for $100,000. The president of Western Union balked, countering that the telephone was nothing but a toy. Two years later, he told colleagues that if he could get the patent for $25&#160;million he would consider it a bargain. By then, the Bell company no longer wanted to sell the patent.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Bell\'s investors would become millionaires while he fared well from residuals and at one point had assets of nearly one million dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell began a series of public demonstrations and lectures to introduce the new invention to the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a> as well as the general public. A short time later, <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Company#Early_promotional_success" title="Bell Telephone Company">his demonstration of an early telephone prototype</a> at the 1876 <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Exposition" title="Centennial Exposition">Centennial Exposition</a> in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> brought the telephone to international attention.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Influential visitors to the exhibition included Emperor <a href="/wiki/Pedro_II_of_Brazil" title="Pedro II of Brazil">Pedro II of Brazil</a>. Later, Bell had the opportunity to demonstrate the invention personally to <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Sir William Thomson</a> (later, Lord Kelvin), a renowned Scottish scientist, as well as to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, who had requested a private audience at <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">Osborne House</a>, her <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> home. She called the demonstration "most extraordinary". The enthusiasm surrounding Bell\'s public displays laid the groundwork for universal acceptance of the revolutionary device.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Company" title="Bell Telephone Company">Bell Telephone Company</a> was created in 1877, and by 1886, more than 150,000 people in the U.S. owned telephones. Bell Company engineers made numerous other improvements to the telephone, which emerged as one of the most successful products ever. In 1879, the Bell company acquired Edison\'s patents for the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_microphone" title="Carbon microphone">carbon microphone</a> from Western Union. This made the telephone practical for longer distances, and it was no longer necessary to shout to be heard at the receiving telephone.\n</p><p>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Pedro_II_of_Brazil" title="Pedro II of Brazil">Pedro II of Brazil</a> was the first person to buy stock in Bell\'s company, the Bell Telephone Company. One of the first telephones in a private residence was installed in his palace in <a href="/wiki/Petr%C3%B3polis" title="Petr\xc3\xb3polis">Petr\xc3\xb3polis</a>, his summer retreat forty miles from <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1915, Bell made the first ceremonial transcontinental <a href="/wiki/Telephone_call" title="Telephone call">telephone call</a>. Calling from the <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation" title="AT&amp;T Corporation">AT&amp;T</a> head office at 15 Dey Street in New York City, Bell was heard by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Watson" title="Thomas A. Watson">Thomas Watson</a> at 333 Grant Avenue in San Francisco. The <i>New York Times</i> reported:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>On October 9, 1876, Alexander Graham Bell and Thomas A. Watson talked by telephone to each other over a two-mile wire stretched between Cambridge and Boston. It was the first wire conversation ever held. Yesterday afternoon [on January 25, 1915], the same two men talked by telephone to each other over a 3,400-mile wire between New York and San Francisco. Dr. Bell, the veteran inventor of the telephone, was in New York, and Mr. Watson, his former associate, was on the other side of the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitors">Competitors</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Parliamentary_Motion_on_Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Canadian Parliamentary Motion on Alexander Graham Bell">Canadian Parliamentary Motion on Alexander Graham Bell</a></div>\n<p>As is sometimes common in scientific discoveries, simultaneous developments can occur, as evidenced by a number of inventors who were at work on the telephone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199919_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199919-114">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Over a period of 18 years, the Bell Telephone Company faced 587 court challenges to its patents, including five that went to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ATCS_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATCS-115">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> but none was successful in establishing priority over the original Bell patent<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595-116">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and the Bell Telephone Company never lost a case that had proceeded to a final trial stage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595-116">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Bell\'s laboratory notes and family letters were the key to establishing a long lineage to his experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroundwater200595-116">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The Bell company lawyers successfully fought off myriad lawsuits generated initially around the challenges by Elisha Gray and <a href="/wiki/Amos_Dolbear" title="Amos Dolbear">Amos Dolbear</a>. In personal correspondence to Bell, both Gray and Dolbear had acknowledged his prior work, which considerably weakened their later claims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay1997179_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay1997179-118">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 13, 1887, the U.S. Government moved to annul the patent issued to Bell on the grounds of fraud and misrepresentation. After a series of decisions and reversals, the Bell company won a decision in the Supreme Court, though a couple of the original claims from the lower court cases were left undecided.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> By the time that the trial wound its way through nine years of legal battles, the U.S. prosecuting attorney had died and the two Bell patents (No. 174,465 dated March 7, 1876, and No. 186,787 dated January 30, 1877) were no longer in effect, although the presiding judges agreed to continue the proceedings due to the case\'s importance as a <a href="/wiki/Stare_decisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Stare decisis">precedent</a>. With a change in administration and charges of conflict of interest (on both sides) arising from the original trial, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">US Attorney General</a> dropped the lawsuit on November 30, 1897, leaving several issues undecided <a href="/wiki/Merit_(legal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Merit (legal)">on the merits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During a deposition filed for the 1887 trial, Italian inventor <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Meucci" title="Antonio Meucci">Antonio Meucci</a> also claimed to have created the first working model of a telephone in Italy in 1834. In 1886, in the first of three cases in which he was involved,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;N 16&#93;</a></sup> Meucci took the stand as a witness in the hopes of establishing his invention\'s priority. Meucci\'s testimony in this case was disputed due to a lack of material evidence for his inventions, as his working models were purportedly lost at the laboratory of <a href="/wiki/ADT_Security_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="ADT Security Services">American District Telegraph</a> (ADT) of New York, which was later incorporated as a subsidiary of Western Union in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-Catania_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catania-123">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ADT_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADT-124">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Meucci\'s work, like many other inventors of the period, was based on earlier acoustic principles and despite evidence of earlier experiments, the final case involving Meucci was eventually dropped upon Meucci\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990271\xe2\x80\x93272_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990271\xe2\x80\x93272-125">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> However, due to the efforts of Congressman <a href="/wiki/Vito_Fossella" title="Vito Fossella">Vito Fossella</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> on June 11, 2002, stated that Meucci\'s "work in the invention of the telephone should be acknowledged".<sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-126">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This did not put an end to the still-contentious issue.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Some modern scholars do not agree with the claims that Bell\'s work on the telephone was influenced by Meucci\'s inventions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bellis_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bellis-130">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;N 17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The value of the Bell patent was acknowledged throughout the world, and patent applications were made in most major countries, but when Bell delayed the German patent application, the electrical firm of <a href="/wiki/Siemens_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens AG">Siemens &amp; Halske (S&amp;H)</a> set up a rival manufacturer of Bell telephones under their own patent. The Siemens company produced near-identical copies of the Bell telephone without having to pay royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay1997178_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay1997178-132">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The establishment of the <a href="/wiki/International_Bell_Telephone_Company" title="International Bell Telephone Company">International Bell Telephone Company</a> in Brussels, Belgium in 1880, as well as a series of agreements in other countries eventually consolidated a global telephone operation. The strain put on Bell by his constant appearances in court, necessitated by the legal battles, eventually resulted in his resignation from the company.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;N 18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_Telephone_Cases" title="The Telephone Cases">The Telephone Cases</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_life">Family life</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A distinguished bearded man, his young elegant wife next to him and their two young daughters poise for a formal portrait" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg/220px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg/330px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg/440px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1873" data-file-height="2418" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Graham Bell, his wife <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel Gardiner Hubbard</a>, and their daughters Elsie (left) and Marian ca. 1885</div></div></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" width="30" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Alexander_Graham_Bell#General_photos_of_or_about_Alexander_Graham_Bell_and_his_family" class="extiw" title="commons:Category:Alexander Graham Bell">Bell and his family members</a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A three-story gray mansion, with a covered front entrance" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg/220px-Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg/330px-Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg/440px-Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3171" data-file-height="2350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brodhead-Bell-Morton_Mansion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Brodhead-Bell mansion, the Bell family residence in Washington, D.C., from 1882 to 1889<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990297\xe2\x80\x93299_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990297\xe2\x80\x93299-135">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>On July 11, 1877, a few days after the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Company" title="Bell Telephone Company">Bell Telephone Company</a> was established, Bell married <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel Hubbard</a> (1857\xe2\x80\x931923) at the Hubbard estate in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>. His wedding present to his bride was to turn over 1,487 of his 1,497 shares in the newly formed Bell Telephone Company.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEber199144_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEber199144-136">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, the newlyweds embarked on a year-long honeymoon in Europe. During that excursion, Bell took a handmade model of his telephone with him, making it a "working holiday". The courtship had begun years earlier; however, Bell waited until he was more financially secure before marrying. Although the telephone appeared to be an "instant" success, it was not initially a profitable venture and Bell\'s main sources of income were from lectures until after 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn199028_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn199028-137">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> One unusual request exacted by his fianc\xc3\xa9e was that he use "Alec" rather than the family\'s earlier familiar name of "Aleck". From 1876, he would sign his name "Alec Bell".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackay1997120_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackay1997120-138">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> They had four children:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Elsie May Bell (1878\xe2\x80\x931964) who married <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Hovey_Grosvenor" title="Gilbert Hovey Grosvenor">Gilbert Hovey Grosvenor</a> of <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic</a> fame.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Marian Hubbard Bell (1880\xe2\x80\x931962) who was referred to as "Daisy". Married <a href="/wiki/David_Fairchild" title="David Fairchild">David Fairchild</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;N 19&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Two sons who died in infancy (Edward in 1881 and Robert in 1883).</li></ul>\n<p>The Bell family home was in Cambridge, Massachusetts, until 1880 when Bell\'s father-in-law bought a house in Washington, D.C.; in 1882 he bought a home in the same city for Bell\'s family, so they could be with him while he attended to the numerous court cases involving patent disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2006202\xe2\x80\x93205_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2006202\xe2\x80\x93205-147">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell was a <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British subject</a> throughout his early life in Scotland and later in Canada until 1882 when he became a <a href="/wiki/Naturalized_citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalized citizen">naturalized citizen</a> of the United States. In 1915, he characterized his status as: "I am not one of those hyphenated Americans who claim allegiance to two countries."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce199090_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce199090-148">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Despite this declaration, Bell has been proudly claimed as a "native son" by all three countries he resided in: the United States, Canada, and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990471\xe2\x80\x93472_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990471\xe2\x80\x93472-149">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1885, a new summer retreat was contemplated. That summer, the Bells had a vacation on <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">Cape Breton Island</a> in Nova Scotia, spending time at the small village of <a href="/wiki/Baddeck,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Baddeck, Nova Scotia">Baddeck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Returning in 1886, Bell started building an estate on a point across from Baddeck, overlooking <a href="/wiki/Bras_d%27Or_Lake" title="Bras d&#39;Or Lake">Bras d\'Or Lake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune200992_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune200992-151">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> By 1889, a large house, christened <i>The Lodge</i> was completed and two years later, a larger complex of buildings, including a new laboratory,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune20092_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune20092-152">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> were begun that the Bells would name <a href="/wiki/Beinn_Bhreagh" title="Beinn Bhreagh">Beinn Bhreagh</a> (Gaelic: <i>beautiful mountain</i>) after Bell\'s ancestral <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish highlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;N 20&#93;</a></sup> Bell also built the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Boatyard" title="Bell Boatyard">Bell Boatyard</a> on the estate, employing up to 40 people building experimental craft as well as wartime lifeboats and workboats for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Navy" title="Royal Canadian Navy">Royal Canadian Navy</a> and pleasure craft for the Bell family. He was an enthusiastic boater, and Bell and his family sailed or rowed a long series of vessels on Bras d\'Or Lake, ordering additional vessels from the <a href="/wiki/H.W._Embree_and_Sons" title="H.W. Embree and Sons">H.W. Embree and Sons</a> boatyard in <a href="/wiki/Port_Hawkesbury,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Port Hawkesbury, Nova Scotia">Port Hawkesbury, Nova Scotia</a>. In his final, and some of his most productive years, Bell split his residency between Washington, D.C., where he and his family initially resided for most of the year, and Beinn Bhreagh, where they spent increasing amounts of time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199922_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod199922-155">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the end of his life, Bell and his family would alternate between the two homes, but <i>Beinn Bhreagh</i> would, over the next 30 years, become more than a summer home as Bell became so absorbed in his experiments that his annual stays lengthened. Both Mabel and Bell became immersed in the Baddeck community and were accepted by the villagers as "their own".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune20092_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune20092-152">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;N 21&#93;</a></sup> The Bells were still in residence at <i>Beinn Bhreagh</i> when the <a href="/wiki/Halifax_Explosion" title="Halifax Explosion">Halifax Explosion</a> occurred on December 6, 1917. Mabel and Bell mobilized the community to help victims in Halifax.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETulloch200642_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETulloch200642-157">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Beinn_Bhreagh,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Beinn Bhreagh, Nova Scotia">Beinn Bhreagh, Nova Scotia</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_inventions">Later inventions</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AG_Bell_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/AG_Bell_1.jpg/220px-AG_Bell_1.jpg" width="220" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/AG_Bell_1.jpg/330px-AG_Bell_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/AG_Bell_1.jpg/440px-AG_Bell_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3894" data-file-height="5406" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AG_Bell_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Graham Bell in his later years</div></div></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" width="30" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Alexander_Graham_Bell#Inventions" class="extiw" title="commons:Category:Alexander Graham Bell">Bell\'s many inventions</a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Although Alexander Graham Bell is most often associated with the invention of the telephone, his interests were extremely varied. According to one of his biographers, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Gray_(author)" title="Charlotte Gray (author)">Charlotte Gray</a>, Bell\'s work ranged "unfettered across the scientific landscape" and he often went to bed voraciously reading the <i>Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia Britannica</i>, scouring it for new areas of interest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2006219_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2006219-158">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The range of Bell\'s inventive genius is represented only in part by the 18 patents granted in his name alone and the 12 he shared with his collaborators. These included 14 for the telephone and telegraph, four for the <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">photophone</a>, one for the <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a>, five for aerial vehicles, four for "hydroairplanes", and two for <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> cells. Bell\'s inventions spanned a wide range of interests and included a metal jacket to assist in breathing, the <a href="/wiki/Audiometer" title="Audiometer">audiometer</a> to detect minor hearing problems, a device to locate icebergs, investigations on how to separate salt from seawater, and work on finding <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a>.\n</p><p>Bell worked extensively in <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">medical research</a> and invented techniques for teaching speech to the deaf. During his <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory</a> period, Bell and his associates considered impressing a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> on a record as a means of reproducing sound. Although the trio briefly experimented with the concept, they could not develop a workable prototype. They abandoned the idea, never realizing they had glimpsed a basic principle which would one day find its application in the <a href="/wiki/Tape_recorder" title="Tape recorder">tape recorder</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hard_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard disc">hard disc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Floppy disc">floppy disc</a> drive, and other <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_medium" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetic medium">magnetic media</a>.\n</p><p>Bell\'s own home used a primitive form of air conditioning, in which fans blew currents of air across great blocks of ice. He also anticipated modern concerns with fuel shortages and industrial pollution. <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> gas, he reasoned, could be produced from the waste of farms and factories. At his Canadian estate in Nova Scotia, he experimented with <a href="/wiki/Composting_toilet" title="Composting toilet">composting toilets</a> and devices to capture water from the atmosphere. In a magazine interview published shortly before his death, he reflected on the possibility of using <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic module">solar panels</a> to heat houses.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photophone">Photophone</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">Photophone</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photophony1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Photophony1.jpg/220px-Photophony1.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Photophony1.jpg/330px-Photophony1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Photophony1.jpg/440px-Photophony1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photophony1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photophone receiver, one half of Bell\'s wireless <a href="/wiki/Optical_communication" title="Optical communication">optical communication</a> system, ca. 1880</div></div></div>\n<p>Bell and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner_Tainter" title="Charles Sumner Tainter">Charles Sumner Tainter</a> jointly invented a wireless telephone, named a <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">photophone</a>, which allowed for the transmission of both sounds and normal human conversations on a beam of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990336_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990336-159">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SDU_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SDU-160">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Both men later became full associates in the <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau#Laboratory_projects" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory Association</a>.\n</p><p>On June 21, 1880, Bell\'s assistant transmitted a wireless voice telephone message a considerable distance, from the roof of the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_School_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Franklin School (Washington, D.C.)">Franklin School</a> in Washington, D.C., to Bell at the window of his laboratory, some 213 metres (700&#160;ft) away, 19 years before the first voice radio transmissions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarson200776\xe2\x80\x9378_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarson200776\xe2\x80\x9378-161">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990338_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990338-162">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell believed the photophone\'s principles were his life\'s "greatest achievement", telling a reporter shortly before his death that the photophone was "the greatest invention [I have] ever made, greater than the telephone".<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The photophone was a precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Fiber-optic_communication" title="Fiber-optic communication">fiber-optic communication</a> systems which achieved popular worldwide usage in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AmericanScientist-1984.V72.No1_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanScientist-1984.V72.No1-167">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Its master patent was issued in December 1880, many decades before the photophone\'s principles came into popular use.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_detector">Metal detector</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot;
