/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Telephone_Inventor%E2%80%99s_Voice_Heard_in_Restored_Recording_%C2%AB_Science_World.theora.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Telephone_Inventor%E2%80%99s_Voice_Heard_in_Restored_Recording_%C2%AB_Science_World.theora.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell\'s voice, from a <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory</a> recording in 1885. Restored by the Smithsonian in 2013.</div></div></div>\n<p>Bell is also credited with developing one of the early versions of a <a href="/wiki/Metal_detector" title="Metal detector">metal detector</a> in 1881. The device was quickly put together in an attempt to find the bullet in the body of U.S. President <a href="/wiki/James_Garfield" class="mw-redirect" title="James Garfield">James Garfield</a>. According to some accounts, the metal detector worked flawlessly in tests but did not find the assassin\'s bullet partly because the metal bed frame on which the President was lying disturbed the instrument, resulting in static.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107-168">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The president\'s surgeons, who were skeptical of the device, ignored Bell\'s requests to move the president to a bed not fitted with metal springs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107-168">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Alternatively, although Bell had detected a slight sound on his first test, the bullet may have been lodged too deeply to be detected by the crude apparatus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997107-168">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell\'s own detailed account, presented to the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> in 1882, differs in several particulars from most of the many and varied versions now in circulation, by concluding that extraneous metal was not to blame for failure to locate the bullet. Perplexed by the peculiar results he had obtained during an examination of Garfield, Bell "proceeded to the Executive Mansion the next morning&#160;... to ascertain from the surgeons whether they were perfectly sure that all metal had been removed from the neighborhood of the bed. It was then recollected that underneath the horse-hair mattress on which the President lay was another mattress composed of steel wires. Upon obtaining a duplicate, the mattress was found to consist of a sort of net of woven steel wires, with large meshes. The extent of the [area that produced a response from the detector] having been so small, as compared with the area of the bed, it seemed reasonable to conclude that the steel mattress had produced no detrimental effect." In a footnote, Bell adds, "The death of President Garfield and the subsequent <i>post-mortem</i> examination, however, proved that the bullet was at too great a distance from the surface to have affected our apparatus."<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrofoils">Hydrofoils</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HD-4" title="HD-4">HD-4</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_HD-4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Bell_HD-4.jpg/220px-Bell_HD-4.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Bell_HD-4.jpg/330px-Bell_HD-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Bell_HD-4.jpg/440px-Bell_HD-4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="445" data-file-height="339" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_HD-4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell <a href="/wiki/HD-4" title="HD-4">HD-4</a> on a test run ca. 1919</div></div></div>\n<p>The March 1906 <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i> article by American pioneer William E. Meacham explained the basic principle of <a href="/wiki/Hydrofoil" title="Hydrofoil">hydrofoils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroplane_(boat)" title="Hydroplane (boat)">hydroplanes</a>. Bell considered the invention of the hydroplane as a very significant achievement. Based on information gained from that article, he began to sketch concepts of what is now called a hydrofoil boat. Bell and assistant <a href="/wiki/Frederick_W._Baldwin" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick W. Baldwin">Frederick W. "Casey" Baldwin</a> began hydrofoil experimentation in the summer of 1908 as a possible aid to airplane takeoff from water. Baldwin studied the work of the Italian inventor <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Forlanini" title="Enrico Forlanini">Enrico Forlanini</a> and began testing models. This led him and Bell to the development of practical hydrofoil watercraft.\n</p><p>During his world tour of 1910\xe2\x80\x9311, Bell and Baldwin met with Forlanini in France. They had rides in the Forlanini hydrofoil boat over <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maggiore" title="Lake Maggiore">Lake Maggiore</a>. Baldwin described it as being as smooth as flying. On returning to Baddeck, a number of initial concepts were built as experimental models, including the <i>Dhonnas Beag</i> (Scottish Gaelic for <i>little devil</i>), the first self-propelled Bell-Baldwin hydrofoil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoileau200418_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoileau200418-170">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The experimental boats were essentially proof-of-concept prototypes that culminated in the more substantial <a href="/wiki/HD-4" title="HD-4">HD-4</a>, powered by <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a> engines. A top speed of 54 miles per hour (87&#160;km/h) was achieved, with the hydrofoil exhibiting rapid acceleration, good stability, and steering, along with the ability to take waves without difficulty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoileau200428\xe2\x80\x9330_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoileau200428\xe2\x80\x9330-171">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 1913, Dr. Bell hired Walter Pinaud, a Sydney yacht designer and builder as well as the proprietor of Pinaud\'s Yacht Yard in <a href="/wiki/Westmount,_Nova_Scotia" title="Westmount, Nova Scotia">Westmount, Nova Scotia</a> to work on the pontoons of the HD-4. Pinaud soon took over the boatyard at Bell Laboratories on Beinn Bhreagh, Bell\'s estate near <a href="/wiki/Baddeck,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Baddeck, Nova Scotia">Baddeck, Nova Scotia</a>. Pinaud\'s experience in boat-building enabled him to make useful design changes to the HD-4. After the First World War, work began again on the HD-4. Bell\'s report to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> permitted him to obtain two 350 horsepower (260 kilowatts) engines in July 1919. On September 9, 1919, the HD-4 set a world marine speed record of 70.86 miles per hour (114.04 kilometres per hour),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoileau200430_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoileau200430-172">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> a record which stood for ten years.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeronautics">Aeronautics</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aerial_Experiment_Association" title="Aerial Experiment Association">Aerial Experiment Association</a> and <a href="/wiki/AEA_Silver_Dart" title="AEA Silver Dart">AEA Silver Dart</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg/220px-AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg/330px-AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg/440px-AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AEA_Silver_Dart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>AEA Silver Dart ca.&#160;1909</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1891, Bell had begun experiments to develop motor-powered heavier-than-air aircraft. The AEA was first formed as Bell shared the vision to fly with his wife, who advised him to seek "young" help as Bell was at the age of 60.\n</p><p>In 1898, Bell experimented with <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetrahedral">tetrahedral</a> <a href="/wiki/Box_kite" title="Box kite">box kites</a> and wings constructed of multiple compound <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_kite" title="Tetrahedral kite">tetrahedral kites</a> covered in maroon silk.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;N 22&#93;</a></sup> The tetrahedral wings were named <i>Cygnet</i> I, II, and III, and were flown both unmanned and manned (<i>Cygnet I</i> crashed during a flight carrying Selfridge) in the period from 1907\xe2\x80\x931912. Some of Bell\'s kites are on display at the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_honors_and_tributes" title="Alexander Graham Bell honors and tributes">Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bell was a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace engineering</a> research through the <a href="/wiki/Aerial_Experiment_Association" title="Aerial Experiment Association">Aerial Experiment Association</a> (AEA), officially formed at Baddeck, Nova Scotia, in October 1907 at the suggestion of his wife <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel</a> and with her financial support after the sale of some of her real estate.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The AEA was headed by Bell and the founding members were four young men: American <a href="/wiki/Glenn_H._Curtiss" class="mw-redirect" title="Glenn H. Curtiss">Glenn H. Curtiss</a>, a motorcycle manufacturer at the time and who held the title "world\'s fastest man", having ridden his self-constructed motor bicycle around in the shortest time, and who was later awarded the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American Trophy</a> for the first official one-kilometre flight in the <a href="/wiki/Western_hemisphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Western hemisphere">Western hemisphere</a>, and who later became a world-renowned airplane manufacturer; Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Selfridge" title="Thomas Selfridge">Thomas Selfridge</a>, an official observer from the U.S. Federal government and one of the few people in the army who believed that aviation was the future; <a href="/wiki/Frederick_W._Baldwin" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick W. Baldwin">Frederick W. Baldwin</a>, the first Canadian and first British subject to pilot a public flight in <a href="/wiki/Hammondsport,_New_York" title="Hammondsport, New York">Hammondsport</a>, New York; and <a href="/w/index.php?title=J._A_.D._McCurdy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="J. A .D. McCurdy (page does not exist)">J. A .D. McCurdy</a>\xe2\x80\x93Baldwin and McCurdy being new engineering graduates from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The AEA\'s work progressed to heavier-than-air machines, applying their knowledge of kites to gliders. Moving to Hammondsport, the group then designed and built the <i><a href="/wiki/AEA_Red_Wing" title="AEA Red Wing">Red Wing</a></i>, framed in bamboo and covered in red silk and powered by a small <a href="/wiki/Engine_cooling" class="mw-redirect" title="Engine cooling">air-cooled</a> engine.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> On March 12, 1908, over <a href="/wiki/Keuka_Lake" title="Keuka Lake">Keuka Lake</a>, the biplane lifted off on the first public flight in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;N 23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;N 24&#93;</a></sup> The innovations that were incorporated into this design included a cockpit enclosure and <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">tail rudder</a> (later variations on the original design would add ailerons as a means of control). One of the AEA\'s inventions, a practical wingtip form of the <a href="/wiki/Aileron" title="Aileron">aileron</a>, was to become a standard component on all aircraft. <sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;N 25&#93;</a></sup> The <i>White Wing</i> and <i>June Bug</i> were to follow and by the end of 1908, over 150 flights without mishap had been accomplished. However, the AEA had depleted its initial reserves and only a <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard#Support_to_aeronautical_research" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">$15,000 grant from Mrs. Bell</a> allowed it to continue with experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips197796_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips197796-182">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Lt. Selfridge had also become the first person killed in a powered heavier-than-air flight in a crash of the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Model_A" title="Wright Model A">Wright Flyer</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myer" title="Fort Myer">Fort Myer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, on September 17, 1908.\n</p><p>Their final aircraft design, the <i><a href="/wiki/AEA_Silver_Dart" title="AEA Silver Dart">Silver Dart</a></i>, embodied all of the advancements found in the earlier machines. On February 23, 1909, Bell was present as the <i>Silver Dart</i> flown by J. A. D. McCurdy from the frozen ice of Bras d\'Or made the first aircraft flight in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Bell had worried that the flight was too dangerous and had arranged for a doctor to be on hand. With the successful flight, the AEA disbanded and the <i>Silver Dart</i> would revert to Baldwin and McCurdy, who began the Canadian Aerodrome Company and would later demonstrate the aircraft to the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Army" title="Canadian Army">Canadian Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips197796\xe2\x80\x9397_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips197796\xe2\x80\x9397-184">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eugenics">Eugenics</span></h2>\n<p>Bell was connected with the <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> movement in the United States. In his lecture <i>Memoir upon the formation of a deaf variety of the human race</i> presented to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> on November 13, 1883, he noted that congenitally deaf parents were more likely to produce deaf children and tentatively suggested that couples where both parties were deaf should not marry.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> However, it was his hobby of livestock breeding which led to his appointment to biologist <a href="/wiki/David_Starr_Jordan" title="David Starr Jordan">David Starr Jordan</a>\'s Committee on Eugenics, under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/American_Breeders%27_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="American Breeders&#39; Association">American Breeders\' Association</a>. The committee unequivocally extended the principle to humans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990410\xe2\x80\x93417_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990410\xe2\x80\x93417-186">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> From 1912 until 1918, he was the chairman of the board of scientific advisers to the <a href="/wiki/Eugenics_Record_Office" title="Eugenics Record Office">Eugenics Record Office</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Cold_Spring_Harbor_Laboratory" title="Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory">Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory</a> in New York, and regularly attended meetings. In 1921, he was the honorary president of the <a href="/wiki/Second_International_Congress_of_Eugenics" class="mw-redirect" title="Second International Congress of Eugenics">Second International Congress of Eugenics</a> held under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> in New York. Organizations such as these advocated passing laws (with success in some states) that established the <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilization" title="Compulsory sterilization">compulsory sterilization</a> of people deemed to be, as Bell called them, a "defective variety of the human race". By the late 1930s, about half the states in the U.S. had eugenics laws, and California\'s compulsory sterilization law was used as a model for that of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_honors">Legacy and honors</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory and Bureau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_honors_and_tributes" title="Alexander Graham Bell honors and tributes">Alexander Graham Bell honors and tributes</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg/220px-Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg/330px-Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg/440px-Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="829" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_Statue_in_front_of_the_Brantford_Bell_Telephone_Building_0.98.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell statue by <a href="/wiki/Cleeve_Horne" title="Cleeve Horne">A.&#160;E. Cleeve Horne</a>, similar in style to the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Memorial" title="Lincoln Memorial">Lincoln Memorial</a>, in the front portico of the Bell Telephone Building of Brantford, Ontario, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Telephone_City" class="mw-redirect" title="The Telephone City">The Telephone City</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;N 26&#93;</a></sup> (Courtesy: <i><b>Brantford Heritage Inventory</b>, City of Brantford, Ontario, Canada</i>)</div></div></div>\n<p>Honors and tributes flowed to Bell in increasing numbers as his invention became ubiquitous and his personal fame grew. Bell received numerous honorary degrees from colleges and universities to the point that the requests almost became burdensome.<sup id="cite_ref-AGB_Family_Papers_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AGB_Family_Papers-191">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> During his life, he also received dozens of major awards, medals, and other tributes. These included statuary monuments to both him and the new form of communication his telephone created, including the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Telephone Memorial">Bell Telephone Memorial</a> erected in his honor in <i>Alexander Graham Bell Gardens</i> in <a href="/wiki/Brantford" title="Brantford">Brantford</a>, Ontario, in 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg/220px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg/330px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg/440px-Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="4288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Graham_Bell_Quote_in_stone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A quote by Alexander Graham Bell engraved in the stone wall within the Peace Chapel of the <a href="/wiki/International_Peace_Garden" title="International Peace Garden">International Peace Garden</a> (in Manitoba Canada and North Dakota, USA).</div></div></div>\n<p>A large number of Bell\'s writings, personal correspondence, notebooks, papers, and other documents reside in both the United States <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress Manuscript Division</a> (as the <i>Alexander Graham Bell Family Papers</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-AGB_Family_Papers_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AGB_Family_Papers-191">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and at the Alexander Graham Bell Institute, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_University" title="Cape Breton University">Cape Breton University</a>, Nova Scotia; major portions of which are available for online viewing.\n</p><p>A number of historic sites and other marks commemorate Bell in North America and Europe, including the first telephone companies in the United States and Canada. Among the major sites are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_National_Historic_Site" title="Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site">Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site</a>, maintained by <a href="/wiki/Parks_Canada" title="Parks Canada">Parks Canada</a>, which incorporates the Alexander Graham Bell Museum, in <a href="/wiki/Baddeck,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Baddeck, Nova Scotia">Baddeck, Nova Scotia</a>, close to the Bell estate <a href="/wiki/Beinn_Bhreagh" title="Beinn Bhreagh">Beinn Bhreagh</a><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site" title="Bell Homestead National Historic Site">Bell Homestead National Historic Site</a>, includes the Bell family home, "Melville House", and farm overlooking Brantford, Ontario and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_River_(Ontario)" title="Grand River (Ontario)">Grand River</a>. It was their first home in North America;</li>\n<li>Canada\'s first telephone company building, the "Henderson Home" of the late 1870s, a predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Canada" title="Bell Canada">Bell Telephone Company of Canada</a> (officially chartered in 1880). In 1969, the building was carefully moved to the historic Bell Homestead National Historic Site in <a href="/wiki/Brantford" title="Brantford">Brantford</a>, Ontario, and was refurbished to become a telephone museum. The Bell Homestead, the Henderson Home telephone museum, and the National Historic Site\'s reception centre are all maintained by the Bell Homestead Society;<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Alexander Graham Bell Memorial Park, which features a broad neoclassical monument built in 1917 by public subscription. The monument depicts mankind\'s ability to span the globe through telecommunications;<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The Alexander Graham Bell Museum (opened in 1956), part of the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_National_Historic_Site" title="Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site">Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site</a> which was completed in 1978 in <a href="/wiki/Baddeck,_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Baddeck, Nova Scotia">Baddeck, Nova Scotia</a>. Many of the museum\'s artifacts were donated by Bell\'s daughters;<br /><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg/220px-Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg/330px-Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg/440px-Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3994" data-file-height="2389" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grahambellmuseumcorridoor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_National_Historic_Site" title="Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site">Bell Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">Cape Breton</a>, part of the Alexander Graham Bell National Historic Site</div></div></div></li></ul>\n<p>In 1880, Bell received the <a href="/wiki/Volta_Prize" title="Volta Prize">Volta Prize</a> with a purse of 50,000 <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">French francs</a> (approximately US$260,000 in today\'s dollars<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-196">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>) for the invention of the telephone from the French government.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-197">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Among the luminaries who judged were <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas,_fils" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandre Dumas, fils">Alexandre Dumas, <i>fils</i>.</a><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2018)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Volta Prize was conceived by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> in 1852, and named in honor of <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a>, with Bell becoming the second recipient of the grand prize in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-204">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Since Bell was becoming increasingly affluent, he used his prize money to create endowment funds (the \'Volta Fund\') and institutions in and around the United States capital of Washington, D.C.. These included the prestigious<i> \'Volta Laboratory Association\' </i>(1880), also known as the<i> <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory</a> </i>and as the<i> \'Alexander Graham Bell Laboratory\', </i>and which eventually led to the Volta Bureau (1887) as a center for studies on deafness which is still in operation in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Washington,_D.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Georgetown, Washington, D.C.">Georgetown, Washington, D.C.</a> The Volta Laboratory became an experimental facility devoted to scientific discovery, and the very next year it improved Edison\'s phonograph by substituting wax for tinfoil as the recording medium and incising the recording rather than indenting it, key upgrades that Edison himself later adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The laboratory was also the site where he and his associate invented his "proudest achievement", "the <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">photophone</a>", the "optical telephone" which presaged <a href="/wiki/Fiber-optic_communication" title="Fiber-optic communication">fibre optical telecommunications</a> while the Volta Bureau would later evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell_Association_for_the_Deaf_and_Hard_of_Hearing" title="Alexander Graham Bell Association for the Deaf and Hard of Hearing">Alexander Graham Bell Association for the Deaf and Hard of Hearing</a> (the AG Bell), a leading center for the research and <a href="/wiki/Pedagogy" title="Pedagogy">pedagogy</a> of deafness.\n</p><p>In partnership with <a href="/wiki/Gardiner_Greene_Hubbard" title="Gardiner Greene Hubbard">Gardiner Greene Hubbard</a>, Bell helped establish the publication <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> during the early 1880s. In 1898, Bell was elected as the second president of the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, serving until 1903, and was primarily responsible for the extensive use of illustrations, including photography, in the magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> He also served for many years as a Regent of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> (1898\xe2\x80\x931922).<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The French government conferred on him the decoration of the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" class="mw-redirect" title="L\xc3\xa9gion d&#39;honneur">L\xc3\xa9gion d\'honneur</a> (Legion of Honor); the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a> in London awarded him the <a href="/wiki/Albert_Medal_(RSA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Medal (RSA)">Albert Medal</a> in 1902; the <a href="/wiki/University_of_W%C3%BCrzburg" title="University of W\xc3\xbcrzburg">University of W\xc3\xbcrzburg</a>, Bavaria, granted him a PhD, and he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute" title="Franklin Institute">Franklin Institute</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Cresson_Medal" title="Elliott Cresson Medal">Elliott Cresson Medal</a> in 1912. He was one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">American Institute of Electrical Engineers</a> in 1884 and served as its president from 1891\xe2\x80\x9392. Bell was later awarded the AIEE\'s <a href="/wiki/Edison_Medal" class="mw-redirect" title="Edison Medal">Edison Medal</a> in 1914 "For meritorious achievement in the invention of the telephone".<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>bel</i> (B) and the smaller <i><a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibel</a></i> (dB) are <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Units of measurement">units of measurement</a> of <a href="/wiki/Level_(logarithmic_quantity)" title="Level (logarithmic quantity)">power level</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> and named after him.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;N 27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Since 1976, the <a href="/wiki/IEEE" class="mw-redirect" title="IEEE">IEEE</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Alexander_Graham_Bell_Medal" title="IEEE Alexander Graham Bell Medal">Alexander Graham Bell Medal</a> has been awarded to honor outstanding contributions in the field of telecommunications.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg/180px-Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg" width="180" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg/270px-Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg/360px-Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="637" data-file-height="678" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Grahm_Bell2_1940_Issue-10c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>~ A.G. Bell issue of 1940 ~</center></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1936, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">US Patent Office</a> declared Bell first on its list of the country\'s greatest inventors,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> leading to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">US Post Office</a> issuing a commemorative stamp honoring Bell in 1940 as part of its <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_United_States#Famous_Americans_Series_of_1940" title="Postage stamps and postal history of the United States">\'Famous Americans Series\'</a>. The First Day of Issue ceremony was held on October 28 in Boston, Massachusetts, the city where Bell spent considerable time on research and working with the deaf. The Bell stamp became very popular and sold out in little time. The stamp became, and remains to this day, the most valuable one of the series.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 150th anniversary of Bell\'s birth in 1997 was marked by a special issue of <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Bank_of_Scotland_%C2%A31_note" title="The Royal Bank of Scotland \xc2\xa31 note">commemorative \xc2\xa31 banknotes</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland#Commemorative_banknotes" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a>. The illustrations on the reverse of the note include Bell\'s face in profile, his signature, and objects from Bell\'s life and career: users of the telephone over the ages; an audio <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">wave signal</a>; a diagram of a telephone receiver; geometric shapes from engineering structures; representations of sign language and the phonetic alphabet; the geese which helped him to understand flight; and the sheep which he studied to understand genetics.<sup id="cite_ref-commemorative_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commemorative-215">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the Government of Canada honored Bell in 1997 with a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mint_numismatic_coins_(20th_century)#100_Dollar_Gold" title="Royal Canadian Mint numismatic coins (20th century)">C$100 gold coin</a>, in tribute also to the 150th anniversary of his birth, and with a silver dollar coin in 2009 in honor of the 100th anniversary of flight in Canada. That first flight was made by an airplane designed under Dr. Bell\'s tutelage, named the Silver Dart.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Bell\'s image, and also those of his many inventions have graced paper money, coinage, and postal stamps in numerous countries worldwide for many dozens of years.\n</p><p>Alexander Graham Bell was ranked 57th among the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a> (2002) in an official BBC nationwide poll,<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> and among the <a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_Canadian" title="The Greatest Canadian">Top Ten Greatest Canadians</a> (2004), and <a href="/wiki/The_Greatest_American" title="The Greatest American">the 100 Greatest Americans</a> (2005). In 2006, Bell was also named as one of the 10 greatest Scottish scientists in history after having been listed in the <a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Scotland" title="National Library of Scotland">National Library of Scotland</a>\'s \'Scottish Science Hall of Fame\'.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Bell\'s name is still widely known and used as part of the names of dozens of educational institutes, corporate namesakes, street and place names around the world.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg/220px-Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg/330px-Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg/440px-Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="527" data-file-height="745" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bell_receives_honorary_LL.D_from_University_of_Edinburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell, an alumnus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>, Scotland, receiving an honorary Doctor of Laws degree (LL.D.) at the university in 1906</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Telephone Memorial">Bell Telephone Memorial</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honorary_degrees">Honorary degrees</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable plainlinks">This list is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Lists#Incomplete_lists" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Lists">incomplete</a>; you can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_Graham_Bell&amp;action=edit">expanding it</a>.</div>\n<p>Alexander Graham Bell, who could not complete the university program of his youth, received at least a dozen honorary degrees from academic institutions, including eight honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">LL.D.s</a> (Doctorate of Laws), two Ph.D.s, a D.Sc., and an M.D.:<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gallaudet_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallaudet College">Gallaudet College</a> (then named National Deaf-Mute College) in Washington, D.C. (Ph.D.) in 1880<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_W%C3%BCrzburg" title="University of W\xc3\xbcrzburg">University of W\xc3\xbcrzburg</a> in W\xc3\xbcrzburg, Bavaria (Ph.D.) in 1882<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a> in Heidelberg, Germany (M.D.) in 1886<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DCB_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCB-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> in Cambridge, Massachusetts (LL.D.) in 1896<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Illinois_College" title="Illinois College">Illinois College</a>, in Jacksonville, Illinois (LL.D.) in 1896, possibly 1881<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Amherst_College" title="Amherst College">Amherst College</a> in Amherst, Massachusetts (LL.D.) in 1901<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">St. Andrew\'s University</a> in St Andrews, Scotland (LL.D) in 1902<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> in Oxford, England (D.Sc.) in 1906<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> in Edinburgh, Scotland (LL.D.) in 1906<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> in Washington, D.C. (LL.D.) in 1913<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University" title="Queen&#39;s University">Queen\'s University</a> in Kingston, Ontario, Canada (LL.D.) in 1908<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> in Hanover, New Hampshire (LL.D.) in 1913,<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> possibly 1914<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162_220-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall1917162-220">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovators_awarded_in_his_name">Innovators awarded in his name</span></h2>\n<ul><li>Aegis Graham Bell Award are consistuted to recognise good work by innovators in India.  Since 2010 awards are being given to innovators in IT and Telecom sector. Companies like Mahendra Tech, Data Infosys, CDOT, Infosys etc. have been awarded for the same.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayal_in_film_and_television">Portrayal in film and television</span></h2>\n<ul><li>The 1939 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="The Story of Alexander Graham Bell">The Story of Alexander Graham Bell</a></i> was based on his life and works.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The 1992 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sound_and_the_Silence" title="The Sound and the Silence">The Sound and the Silence</a></i> was a TV film.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Biography_(TV_series)" title="Biography (TV series)">Biography</a></i> aired an episode <i>Alexander Graham Bell: Voice of Invention</i> on 6 August 1996.</li>\n<li><i>Eyewitness No. 90 A Great Inventor Is Remembered</i>, a 1957 <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Board_of_Canada" title="National Film Board of Canada">NFB</a> short about Bell.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>\n<p>Bell died of complications arising from <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> on August 2, 1922, at his private estate in Cape Breton, Nova Scotia, at age 75.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2006419_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2006419-226">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Bell had also been afflicted with <a href="/wiki/Pernicious_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pernicious anemia">pernicious anemia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2006418_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2006418-227">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> His last view of the land he had inhabited was by moonlight on his mountain estate at 2:00&#160;a.m.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;N 28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune2009119_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune2009119-231">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;N 29&#93;</a></sup> While tending to him after his long illness, Mabel, his wife, whispered, "Don\'t leave me." By way of reply, Bell signed "no...", lost consciousness, and died shortly after.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-197">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990491_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990491-233">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On learning of Bell\'s death, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Canada" title="Prime Minister of Canada">Canadian Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lyon_Mackenzie_King" title="William Lyon Mackenzie King">Mackenzie King</a>, cabled Mrs. Bell, saying:<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-197">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>My colleagues in the Government join with me in expressing to you our sense of the world\'s loss in the death of your distinguished husband. It will ever be a source of pride to our country that the great invention, with which his name is immortally associated, is a part of its history. On the behalf of the citizens of Canada, may I extend to you an expression of our combined gratitude and sympathy.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Bell\'s coffin was constructed of Beinn Bhreagh pine by his laboratory staff, lined with the same red silk fabric used in his tetrahedral kite experiments. To help celebrate his life, his wife asked guests not to wear black (the traditional funeral color) while attending his service, during which soloist Jean MacDonald sang a verse of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>\'s "Requiem":<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune2009119\xe2\x80\x93120_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune2009119\xe2\x80\x93120-234">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Under a wide and starry sky,</dd>\n<dd>Dig the grave and let me lie.</dd>\n<dd>Glad did I live and gladly die</dd>\n<dd>And I laid me down with a will.</dd></dl>\n<p>Upon the conclusion of Bell\'s funeral, "every phone on the continent of North America was silenced in honor of the man who had given to mankind the means for direct communication at a distance".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune20092_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune20092-152">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsborne194318\xe2\x80\x9319_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsborne194318\xe2\x80\x9319-235">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dr. Alexander Graham Bell was buried atop Beinn Bhreagh mountain, on his estate where he had resided increasingly for the last 35 years of his life, overlooking Bras d\'Or Lake.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_197-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-197">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> He was survived by his wife <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel</a>, his two daughters, Elsie May and Marian, and nine of his grandchildren.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_197-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-197">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See Francis Channing">William Francis Channing</a>, submitted telephone ideas to Bell</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>[Is the following a quote from the source referenced?:</i>] While Bell worked in many scientific, technical, professional and social capacities throughout his life he would remain fondest of his earliest vocation. To the end of his days, when discussing himself, Bell would always add with pride "I am a teacher of the deaf".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bell was a British citizen for most of his early life. When he moved to Canada in 1870, Canadian and British citizenship were functionally identical, with <a href="/wiki/Canadian_nationality_law" title="Canadian nationality law">Canadian citizenship</a> only becoming a formal classification in 1910. He applied for American citizenship after 1877, gained it in 1882, and referred to himself as an American citizen from that point on. Quote from Bell speaking to his wife: "you are a citizen because you can\'t help it \xe2\x80\x93 you were born one, but I chose to be one."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray2006228_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2006228-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Aside from Bell\'s own view of his citizenship, many, if not most Canadians considered him also as one of theirs as evidenced in an address by the Governor General of Canada. On October 24, 1917, in Brantford, Ontario, the Governor General spoke at the unveiling of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Telephone Memorial">Bell Telephone Memorial</a> to an audience numbering in the thousands, saying: "Dr. Bell is to be congratulated upon being able to receive the recognition of his fellow citizens and fellow countrymen".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From <a href="#CITEREFBlack1997">Black (1997)</a>, p.&#160;18: "He thought he could harness the new <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic technology</a> by creating a machine with a transmitter and receiver that would send sounds telegraphically to help people hear."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After Bell\'s death his wife Mabel wrote to <a href="/wiki/John_J._Carty" title="John J. Carty">John J. Carty</a>, an AT&amp;T vice-president, and commented on her husband\'s reluctance to have a phone in his study, saying "[of the statements in the newspapers] ...publishing of Mr. Bell\'s dislike of the telephone. Of course, he never had one in his study. That was where he went when he wanted to be alone with his thoughts and his work. The telephone, of course, means intrusion by the outside world. And the little difficulties and delays often attending the establishment of conversation... did irritate him, so that as a rule he preferred having others send and receive messages. But all really important business over the telephone he transacted himself. There are few private houses more completely equipped with telephones than ours... and there was nothing that Mr. Bell was more particular about than our telephone service... We never could have come here [to Beinn Bhreagh] in the first place or continued here, but for the telephone which kept us in close touch with doctors and neighbors and the regular telegraph office... Mr. Bell did like to say in fun, "Why did I ever invent the Telephone," but no one had a higher appreciation of its indispensableness or used it more freely when need was\xe2\x80\x94either personally or by deputy \xe2\x80\x94and he was really tremendously proud of it and all it was accomplishing."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Bell typically signed his name in full on his correspondence.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Helmholtz\'s <i>The Sensations of Tone</i> is credited with inspiring Bell, at the age of 23, to further his studies of electricity and electromagnetism.<sup id="cite_ref-DCB_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCB-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The family pet was given to his brother\'s family.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The estate, dating from 1858, is in the present day located at 94 Tutela Heights Road, Brantford, and is now known as the "Bell Homestead", and formally as the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Homestead_National_Historic_Site" title="Bell Homestead National Historic Site">Bell Homestead National Historic Site of Canada</a>. It received its historical designation from the Government of Canada on 1 June 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Bell would later write that he had come to Canada a "dying man".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bell was thrilled at his recognition by the Six Nations Reserve and throughout his life would launch into a Mohawk war dance when he was excited.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In later years, Bell described the invention of the telephone and linked it to his "dreaming place".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> <a href="#CITEREFEber1991">Eber (1991)</a>, p.&#160;43 claimed that Mabel suffered scarlet fever in New York "<i>...shortly before her fifth birthday...</i>"; however, <a href="#CITEREFToward1984">Toward (1984)</a> provided a detailed chronology of the event claiming "...&#160;shortly after their arrival in New York [in January 1863]" when Mabel would have been at least five years and five weeks of age. Mabel\'s exact age when she became deaf would later play a part in the debate on the effectiveness of <a href="/wiki/Manualism_and_oralism" class="mw-redirect" title="Manualism and oralism">manual versus oral education for deaf children</a>, as children who are older at the onset of deafness retain greater vocalization skills and are thus more successful in oral education programs. Some of the debate centred on whether Mabel had to relearn oral speech from scratch, or whether she never lost it.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> From <a href="#CITEREFAlexander_Graham_Bell1979"><i>Alexander Graham Bell</i> (1979)</a>, p.&#160;8: "Brantford is justified in calling herself \'The Telephone City\' because the telephone originated there. It was invented in Brantford at Tutela Heights in the summer of 1874."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Hubbard\'s financial support to the research efforts fell far short of the funds needed, necessitating Bell to continue teaching while conducting his experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Bell was so short of funds at times that he had to borrow money from his own employee, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Watson" title="Thomas A. Watson">Thomas Watson</a>. Bell also sought an additional CAD$150 from the former <a href="/wiki/Premier_(Canada)" title="Premier (Canada)">Premier of Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Brown_(Canadian_politician)" title="George Brown (Canadian politician)">George Brown</a>, in exchange for 50% of the patent rights in the British Empire (Brown later retracted his offer to patent the telephone in the U.K. for fear of being ridiculed). The <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Company#Predecessor_to_the_Bell_Company" title="Bell Telephone Company">Bell Patent Association</a>, composed of Hubbard, Sanders and Bell and which would become the precursor of the Bell Telephone Company (and later, <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation" title="AT&amp;T Corporation">AT&amp;T</a>), would later assign an approximate 10% interest of its shares to Watson,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1990291_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1990291-93">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> in lieu of salary and for his earlier financial support to Bell while they worked together creating their first functional telephone.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A copy of a draft of the patent application is shown, described as "probably the most valuable patent ever."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Meucci was not involved in the final trial.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="What final trial is this talking about? (June 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tomas Farley also writes that "Nearly every scholar agrees that Bell and Watson were the first to transmit intelligible speech by electrical means. Others transmitted a sound or a click or a buzz but our boys [Bell and Watson] were the first to transmit speech one could understand."<sup id="cite_ref-Bellis_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bellis-130">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Many of the lawsuits became rancorous, with Elisha Gray becoming particularly bitter over Bell\'s ascendancy in the telephone debate, but Bell refused to launch a countersuit for libel.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marian was born only days after Bell and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner_Tainter" title="Charles Sumner Tainter">Sumner Tainter</a> had successfully tested their new wireless telecommunication invention at their <a href="/wiki/Volta_Laboratory_and_Bureau" title="Volta Laboratory and Bureau">Volta Laboratory</a>, one which Bell would name as his greatest achievement. Bell was so ecstatic that he wanted to jointly name his new invention and his new daughter <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">Photophone</a> (Greek: "<i>light\xe2\x80\x93sound</i>"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997104_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997104-144">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Bell wrote: "Only think!\xe2\x80\x94Two babies in one week! Mabel\'s baby was light enough at birth but mine was LIGHT ITSELF! Mabel\'s baby screamed inarticulately but mine spoke with distinct enunciation from the first." Bell\'s suggested scientific name for their new infant daughter did not go over well with Marian\'s mother, <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Gardiner_Hubbard" title="Mabel Gardiner Hubbard">Mabel Gardiner Hubbard Bell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997104_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrosvenorWesson1997104-144">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Under the direction of the Boston architects, <a href="/wiki/Cabot,_Everett_%26_Mead" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabot, Everett &amp; Mead">Cabot, Everett &amp; Mead</a>, a Nova Scotia company, Rhodes, Curry &amp; Company, carried out the actual construction.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> In one memorable incident, the newly arrived Bells were walking down one of Baddeck\'s central streets when Bell peered into a storefront window and saw a frustrated shopkeeper fiddling with his problematic telephone. Bell quickly disassembled it and effected a repair, to the owner\'s amazement. When asked how he was able to do so Bell only needed to introduce himself.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bell was inspired in part by Australian aeronautical engineer <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Hargrave" title="Lawrence Hargrave">Lawrence Hargrave</a>\'s work with man-carrying box kites.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Hargrave declined to take patents on his inventions, similar to Bell\'s decision not to file patents on some of his inventions. Bell also chose maroon-colored silk as it would show up clearly against the light-colored sky in his photographic studies.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Selfridge Aerodrome Sails Steadily for 319 feet (97&#160;m)." <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> May 13, 1908.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> At 25 to 30 Miles an Hour. First Public Trip of Heavier-than-air Car in America. Professor Alexander Graham Bell\'s New Machine, Built After Plans by Lieutenant Selfridge, Shown to Be Practicable by Flight Over <a href="/wiki/Keuka_Lake" title="Keuka Lake">Keuka Lake</a>. Portion of Tail Gives Way, Bringing the Test to an End. Views of an Expert. <a href="/wiki/Hammondsport,_New_York" title="Hammondsport, New York">Hammondsport</a>, New York, March 12, 1908.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-181"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-181">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The aileron had been conceived of as early as 1868 by British inventor <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Piers_Watt_Boulton" title="Matthew Piers Watt Boulton">M.P.W. Boulton</a> and was also created independently by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Esnault-Pelterie" title="Robert Esnault-Pelterie">Robert Esnault-Pelterie</a> and several others.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-190">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\nThe <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fleetford_Sise" title="Charles Fleetford Sise">Charles Fleetford Sise</a> Chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Pioneers,_a_Volunteer_Network" title="Pioneers, a Volunteer Network">Telephone Pioneers of America</a> commissioned and dedicated the large bronze statue of Bell in the front portico of <a href="/wiki/Brantford" title="Brantford">Brantford</a>, Ontario\'s new Bell Telephone Building plant on June 17, 1949. Attending the formal ceremony were Bell\'s daughter, Mrs. Gillbert Grosvenor, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Johnson_(businessman)" title="Frederick Johnson (businessman)">Frederick Johnson</a>, President of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Canada" title="Bell Canada">Bell Telephone Company of Canada</a>, T.N. Lacy, President of the Telephone Pioneers, and Brantford Mayor Walter J. Dowden. To each side of the portico facing the monument are the engraved inscriptions "In Grateful Recognition of the Inventor of the Telephone". Its dedication was broadcast live nationally by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">Canadian Broadcasting Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-211">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The decibel is defined as one tenth of a bel.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-230"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-230">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the last years of his life, as his final projects wound down, Bell and his wife, their extended family and friends, lived exclusively at their beloved Beinn Bhreagh.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethune200995_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethune200995-228">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-232"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-232">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From <a href="#CITEREFBethune2009">Bethune (2009)</a>, p.&#160;119: "[his end came] at 2:00&#160;am... His wife, Mabel, daughter Daisy, and son-in-law David Fairchild had gathered around him. His last view was of the moon rising above the mountain he loved".</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGray2006" class="citation book">Gray, Charlotte (2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ujlCgf1uSJIC&amp;pg=PA151"><i>Reluctant Genius: The Passionate Life and Inventive Mind of Alexander Graham Bell</i></a>. Brantford, Ontario: Brantford Historical Society &amp; Hurley Printing. p.&#160;319<span class="reference-accessdate">.
